A. Intro

Pull the files for lab 7 from the skeleton.

git fetch shared
git merge shared/lab7 -m "Start lab7"
git push

Learning Goals

In this lab, we consider ways of measuring the efficiency of a given cod= e segment. Given a function f, we want to find out how fast th= at function runs. One way of doing this is to take out a stopwatch and meas= uring exactly how much time it takes for f to run on some inpu= t. We will look at some graphs of runtime to understand how some function's= runtimes change with the size of their input. However, we'll also see that= there are problems with using elapsed time for this purpose.

Then, we'll learn how to develop better models for analyzing runtime com= plexity. Oftentimes, the programs we need to analyze are too complex to sol= ve using basic counting rules so we need some way of organizing information= and reducing the complexity of the problem. In this last section, we'll le= arn how we can break complex algorithms down into smaller pieces that we ca= n then individually analyze.

B. Estimating efficiency by= timing it

Algorithms

An algorithm is an abstract notion that describes an approach f= or solving a problem. The code we write in this class, our programs, are im= plementations of algorithms.

For example, consider the problem of sorting a list of numbers. One algo= rithm we might use to solve this problem is called bubble sort. Bu= bble sort tells us we can sort a list by repeatedly looping through the lis= t and swapping adjacent items if they are out of order, until the entire so= rting is complete.

Another algorithm we might use to solve this problem is called inser= tion sort. Insertion sort says to sort a list by looping through our l= ist, taking out each item we find, and putting it into a new list in the co= rrect order.

Several websites like VisuAlgo, Sorting.at, Sorting Algorithms, and US= F have developed some animations that can help us visualize these sorti= ng algorithms. Spend a little time playing around with these demos to get a= n understanding of how much time it takes for bubble sort or insertion sort= to completely sort a list. We'll revisit sorting in more detail later in t= his course, but for now, try to get a feeling of how long each algorithm ta= kes to sort a list. How many comparison does each sort need? And how many s= waps?

Since each comparison and each swap takes time, we want to know which is= the faster algorithm: bubble sort or insertion sort? And how fast can we m= ake our Java programs that implement them?

Time Estimates

To start, it seems that the most reasonable way to estimate the time an = algorithm takes is to measure the time directly. Each computer has an inter= nal clock that keeps track of time, usually in the number of fractions of a= second that have elapsed since a given base date. The Java method that acc= esses the clock is System.nanoTime(). A timing.Timer class is provided in timing.Timer.java.

Take 5 minutes or less now to find out

  • exactly what value System.nanoTime() returns, and
  • how to use the timing.Timer class to measure the time ta= ken by a given segment of code.

Exercise: Measuring So= me Sorting Functions

The file timing.Sorter.java contains multiple implementatio= ns of various sorting algorithms, including the bubble sort and insertion s= ort algorithms mentioned earlier. Its getRandomArray method returns an array of the specified si= ze filled with randomly generated values.

The file timing.GraphSorters contains some code to generate= a plot of the run-times of different sorting implementations, sorting diff= erent sizes of arrays. Its main method accepts three arguments (or sets them by default) correspon= ding to the number of different array sizes it should test (ntrials), the interval by which it should increase the array size between trials (by), and the number of times it should repeat each size array per= sorting implementation (nrepeats). By default, the minimum size array is 5.

For example, the commands

javac timing/GraphSorters.java
java timing.GraphSorters 100 50 10

uses the command-line arguments to plot all sorters' runtimes for 100 tr= ials, each increasing by 50 elements, and repeating each trial 10 times (an= d averaging the result).

Try running the program, either with IntelliJ or over the commandline. T= o run with IntelliJ, you can simply run the main method of GraphSorte= rs. Play with the values of ntrials, by, and nr= epeats to see how the graphs change!

Discussion: Timing Results

How fast is timing.Sorter.java? As you can see, this answer= clearly depends on the sorting algorithm, as well as the size of the array= .

Is one sorting algorithm always faster than another? Does incre= asing the array size always mean the sorting takes longer?

How does changing nrepeats change the plot?

Is your plot the exact same as your neighbors plot, even with t= he same values of ntrials, by, and nrepeats= ?

Look at the source code for timing.BubbleSorter to ti= ming.WipingBubbleSorter. After looking at the plots, can you intuiti= vely explain why timing.WipingBubbleSorter is usually 2x as fast as timin= g.BubbleSorter?

While timing how long a program runs is certainly useful, it doesn't giv= e us a reliable answer - there are too many variables.

C. Abbreviated Estimates

Estimation with Proportional To<= /h4>

Instead of timing how long a program segment takes, we can say that a pr= ogram segment runs in time proportional to a given expression, whe= re that expression is in simplest possible terms. Here, proportional to= means within a constant multiple of.

Thus 2N+3" role=3D"presentat= ion" style=3D"position: relative;">2N+3= 2N+3 is "proportional = to N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= NN" since it's = approximately 2&#x00D7;N" role=3D"pr= esentation" style=3D"position: relative;">2=C3=97N= 2=C3=97N. Likewise, <= span class=3D"MathJax" id=3D"MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex=3D"0" data-m= athml=3D"3= N5+19N4+35" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= 3N5+19N+353N5+19N4+35<= /math> is "proportional to N5" role=3D"presentat= ion" style=3D"position: relative;">N5= N5" since it's approximately <= span class=3D"MathJax" id=3D"MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex=3D"0" data-m= athml=3D"3= &#x00D7;N5" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"po= sition: relative;">3=C3=97= N53=C3=97N5. As N= " role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">NN approaches infinity, the= approximation better models the function's real runtime.

Basically, what we're doing here is discarding all but the most signific= ant term of the estimate and also discarding any constant factor of that te= rm. In a later section, we can show exactly why this holds with a more form= al notation.

    Orders of = Growth are Proportional to a Given Variable

    Unfortunately, students are sometimes careless about specifying the quan= tity on which an estimate depends. Don't just use N" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= NN without making clear what N<= /mi>" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= NN means. This disti= nction is important especially when we begin to touch on sorting later in t= he course. It may not always be clear what N" role=3D"pr= esentation" style=3D"position: relative;">NN means.

    Commonly-Occurring Orders of= Growth

    Here are some commonly-occurring estimates listed from no growth at all = to fastest growth.

    • constant time
    • logarithmic time or proportional to l= og&#x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" style= =3D"position: relative;">logN= log=E2=81=A1N
    • linear time or proportional to N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mi" id=3D"MathJax-Span-803" style=3D"font-family: MathJax_Mat= h-italic;">NN
    • quadratic/polynomial time or proportional to = N2" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span style=3D"position: absolute; clip: rect(1.148em, 1001.41em, 2.294em, -= 999.997em); top: -2.133em; left: 0em;">N2N2
    • exponential time or proportional to kN" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">kN= = k<= /mi>N<= /span>
    • factorial time or proportional to N!" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= nobr aria-hidden=3D"true">= N!N!<= /span>

    Logarithmic Algorithms

    We will shortly encounter algorithms that run in time proportional to = log&#x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" s= tyle=3D"position: relative;">= logNlog=E2=81=A1N for some s= uitably defined N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"positio= n: relative;">N<= math xmlns=3D"http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N<= /span>. Recall from algebra that the base-10 logarithm of a value is the ex= ponent to which 10 must be raised to produce the value. It is usually abbre= viated as log10" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: rel= ative;">log= 10<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.138em;"><= /span><= /span>log10. Thus

    • log10<= /mn>&#x2061;1000" role=3D"presen= tation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mi" id=3D"MathJax-Span-840" style=3D"font-family: MathJax_Mai= n;">log101000log<= mn>10=E2=81=A11000 is 3 because 103=3D1000" role=3D"presenta= tion" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mn" id=3D"MathJax-Span-849" style=3D"font-family: MathJax_Mai= n;">103<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.013em;"><= /span>=3D1000103= =3D1000
    • log10<= /mn>&#x2061;90" role=3D"presenta= tion" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mi" id=3D"MathJax-Span-858" style=3D"font-family: MathJax_Mai= n;">log1090log= 10=E2=81=A190 is = slightly less than 2 because 102=3D100" rol= e=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">102=3D100102=3D100
    • log10<= /mn>&#x2061;1" role=3D"presentat= ion" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mi" id=3D"MathJax-Span-876" style=3D"font-family: MathJax_Mai= n;">log101log1= 0=E2=81=A11 is 0 = because 100<= /mn>=3D1" role=3D"presentation" styl= e=3D"position: relative;">100=3D1100= =3D1

    In algorithms, we commonly deal with the base-2 logarithm, written as = lg" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">lglg, defined similarly.

    Another way to think of log is the following: logbase&a= mp;#x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: = relative;">logbaseNlogbase=E2=81=A1N is the number of times N" role=3D"presentation= " style=3D"position: relative;">NN<= /mi> must be divided by base" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span style=3D"position: absolute; clip: rect(1.305em, 1001.83em, 2.294em, -= 999.997em); top: -2.133em; left: 0em;">basebase before it h= its 1. For the purposes of determining orders of growth, however, the log b= ase actually doesn't make a difference because, by the change of base formu= la, we know that any logarithm of N" role=3D"presentation= " style=3D"position: relative;">NN<= /mi> is within a constant factor of any other logarith= m of N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relativ= e;">NN. We= usually express a logarithmic algorithm as simply log&#= x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: rela= tive;">logN= = log=E2= =81=A1N as a result.

    Algorithms for which the running time is logarithmic are those where pro= cessing discards a large proportion of values in each iterations. The binar= y search algorithm is an example. We can use binary search in order to gues= s a number that a person in thinking. In each iteration, the algorithm disc= ards half the possible values for the searched-for number, repeatedly divid= ing the size of the problem by 2 until there is only one value left.

    For example, say you started with a range of 1024 numbers in the number = guessing game. Each time you would discard half of the numbers so that each= round would have the following numbers under consideration:

    Round # Numbers left
    1 1024
    2 512
    3 256
    4 128
    5 64
    6 32
    7 16
    8 8
    9 4
    10 2
    11 1

    We know from above that lg&#x2061;1024<= mo>=3D10" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: rel= ative;">lg1024= =3D10<= span class=3D"MJX_Assistive_MathML" role=3D"presentation">lg=E2=81=A11024=3D10 which gives us an approximati= on of how many rounds it will take.

    We will see further applications of logarithmic behavior when we work wi= th trees in subsequent activities.

    D. Modeling runtime analysis

    A Graphical Approach= to Analyzing Iteration

    We've thus far defined the language of asymptotic analysis and developed= some simple methods based on counting the total number of steps. However, = the kind of problems we want to solve are often too complex to think of jus= t in terms of number iterations times however much work is done per iterati= on.

    Consider the following function, repeatedSum.

    long repeatedSum(int[] values)=
 {
    int N =3D values.length;
    long sum =3D 0;
    for (i=
nt i =3D 0; i < N; i++) {
        for (int j =3D i; j < N; j++) {
            sum +=3D values[j];
        }
    }
    return sum;
}

    In repeatedSum, we're given an array of values= of length N. We want to take the repeated sum over the array as defined by= the following sequence of j's.

    0,1,2,3,&#x22EF;,N&#x2= 212;1" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relati= ve;">0,1,2,3,=E2=8B= =AF,N=E2=88=9210,1,2<= mo>,3,=E2=8B=AF,N=E2= =88=921

    1,2,3,4,&#x22EF;,N&#x2212;= 1" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span style=3D"position: absolute; clip: rect(1.357em, 1008.08em, 2.555em, -= 999.997em); top: -2.185em; left: 0em;">1,2,3,4,=E2=8B=AF,N=E2=88=9211,2,3,4,=E2=8B=AF,N=E2=88=921

    2,3,4,5,&#x22EF;,N&#x2212;= 1" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span style=3D"position: absolute; clip: rect(1.357em, 1008.08em, 2.555em, -= 999.997em); top: -2.185em; left: 0em;">2,3,4,5,= =E2=8B=AF,N=E2= =88=9212,3,4,5,=E2=8B=AF,N= =E2=88=921

    Notice that each time, the number of elements, or the iterations of j, being added is reduced by 1. While in the first iteration, we s= um over all N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: = relative;">N<= math xmlns=3D"http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N<= /span> elements, in the second iteration, we only sum over N&#x2212;1" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"positio= n: relative;">N=E2=88=921N= =E2=88=921 elements. On the next iterati= on, even fewer: just N&#x2212;2" r= ole=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">=E2=88=922N=E2=88=922 elements. This pattern continues until the outer loop, i, has incremented all the way to N" role=3D"present= ation" style=3D"position: relative;">N= N.

    One possible approach to this problem is to draw a bar graph to visualiz= e how much work is being done for each iteration of i. We can = represent this by plotting the values of i across the X-axis o= f the graph and the number of steps for each corresponding value of i= across the Y-axis of the graph.

    Now, let's plot the amount of work being done on the first iteration of = i where i =3D 0. If we examine this iteration alo= ne, we just need to measure the amount of work done by the j l= oop. In this case, the j loop does work proportional to <= span class=3D"MathJax" id=3D"MathJax-Element-106-Frame" tabindex=3D"0" data= -mathml=3D"N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">NN steps as th= e loop starts at 0, increments by 1, and only terminates when j =3D N= .

    How about the next iteration of i? The loop starts at 1 now= instead of 0 but still terminates at N" role=3D"present= ation" style=3D"position: relative;">N= N. In this case, the j loop is= proportional to N&#x2212;1" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= N= =E2=88=921N=E2=88=921 steps. The next loop, then, is proportional to N&#= x2212;2" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: rela= tive;">N=E2=88=922N=E2=88= =922 steps.

    We can start to see a pattern forming. As i increases by 1,= the amount of work done on each corresponding j loop decrease= s by 1. As i approaches N" role=3D"present= ation" style=3D"position: relative;">N= N, the number of steps in the j loop approaches 0. In the final iteration, i =3D N-1, the j loop performs work proportional to 1 step.

    We've now roughly measured each loop proportional to some number of step= s. Each independent bar represents the amount of work any one iteration of = i will perform. The runtime of the entire function repea= tedSum then is the sum of all the bars, or simply the area underneat= h the line.

    The problem is now reduced to finding the area of a triangle with a base= of N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relativ= e;">NN and height of also N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"p= osition: relative;">NN. Thus, the runtime of repeatedSum is in <= span class=3D"MathJax" id=3D"MathJax-Element-113-Frame" tabindex=3D"0" data= -mathml=3D"&#x0398;(N2)" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= =CE=98(N2)=CE=98(N2).

    We can verify this result mathematically by noticing that the sequence c= an be described by the following summation:

    1+2+3+...+N=3DN(N+1)2" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= 1+2+3+...+N=3DN(N+1= )2= 1+2+= 3+...+N= =3DN(N+= 1)2 or, roughly, N22" ro= le=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">N= 2<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.013em;"><= /span>2= = N= 22 which is in = &#x0398;(N2)" ro= le=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">=CE= =98(N= 2)=CE=98(N2= ). It's usef= ul to know both the formula as well as its derivation through the graph abo= ve.

    Tree Recursion

    Now that we've learned how to use a bar graph to represent the runtime o= f an iterative function, let's try the technique out on a recursive functio= n, mitosis.

    int mitosis(int N) {
    if (N =3D=3D 1) {
        return 1;
    }
    return mitosis(N / 2) + mitosis(N / 2);
}

    Let's start by trying to map each N" role=3D"present= ation" style=3D"position: relative;">N= N over the x-axis like we did before and tr= y to see how much work is done for each call to the function. The condition= al contributes a constant amount of work to each call. But notice that in o= ur return statement, we make two recursive calls to mitosis. H= ow do we represent the runtime for these calls? We know that each call to <= code>mitosis does a constant amount of work evaluating the condition= al base case but it's much more difficult to model exactly how much work ea= ch recursive call will do. While a bar graph is a very useful way of repres= enting the runtime of iterative functions, it's not always the right tool f= or recursive functions.

    Instead, let's try another strategy: drawing call trees. Like the graphi= cal approach we used for iteration earlier, the call tree will red= uce the complexity of the problem and allow us to find the overall runtime = of the program on large values of N" role=3D"presentatio= n" style=3D"position: relative;"><= span class=3D"mrow" id=3D"MathJax-Span-1114">NN by taking the tree recursion out of the proble= m. Consider the call tree for fib below.

    int fib(int N) =
{
    if (N <=3D 1) {
        return 1;
    }
    return fib(n - 1) + fib(n - 2);
}

    At the root of the tree, we make our first call to fib(n)= . The recursive calls to fib(n - 1) and fib(n - 2= ) are modeled as the two children of the root node. We say = that this tree has a branching factor of two as each node contains= two children. It takes a constant number of instructions to evaluate the c= onditional, addition operator, and the return statement as denoted by the <= code>1.

    We can see this pattern occurs for all nodes in the tree: each node perf= orms the same constant number of operations if we don't consider recursive = calls. If we can come up with a scheme to count all the nodes, then we can = simply multiply by the constant number of operations to find the overall ru= ntime of fib.

    Remember that the number of nodes in a tree is calculated as the branchi= ng factor, k" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: r= elative;">kk, raised to = the height of the tree, h" role=3D"presentation" style= =3D"position: relative;">hh, or kh<= /mi>" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relativ= e;">khkh. Spend a little time thinking about the maximum height of = this tree: when does the base case tell us the tree recursion will stop?

    Self-test: Choosing the Ri= ght Notation

    Together, we are going to identify the asymptotic runtime complexity of = fib(N).

    • Try 1: &#x0398;(N2)" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">=CE=98(N2)=CE=98(N2)

      • This is incorrect. Each instance of fib makes two recu= rsive calls to fib again. This continues for N levels.

    • Try 2: O(<= mi>N2= )" role=3D"presentation" style= =3D"position: relative;">O(N2)O(N2)<= /span>

      • Again incorrect for the same reason - each instance of fib makes two recursive calls to fib again. This continues fo= r N levels.

    • Try 3: &#x0398;(2N)" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">=CE=98(2N)<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.3= 72em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.503em;"><= /nobr>=CE=98<= /mi>(2N)

      • Correct! There's a small but significant difference between big-Oh = and big-Theta in this problem as the runtime for fib is not ex= actly &#x0398;(2N)" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">=CE=98(2N)=CE=98(2= N) as g= iven by the tight bound, theta. The call tree is actually imbalanced as the= left edge contains N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"po= sition: relative;">NN= nodes but the right edge contains only N/2" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= N<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: = 0.107em;">/2N/2<= /span> nodes. We describe this runtime more exactly as &#x0398;(1.618N)" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= =CE=98(1.618N)=CE=98(1.618= N) whic= h is within O(2= N)" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"= position: relative;">O(2NO(2N) but not within &#x0398;(2N)" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"po= sition: relative;">=CE=98(2N)=CE=98(2<= mrow class=3D"MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">N).

    Now, back to mitosis. The problem is setup just like = fib except instead of decrementing N" role=3D"p= resentation" style=3D"position: relative;">NN by 1 or 2, we now divide N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= NN in half each tim= e. Each node performs a constant amount of work but also makes two recursiv= e calls to mitosis(N / 2).

    Like before, we want to identify both the branching factor and the heigh= t of the tree. In this case, the branching factor is 2 like before. Recall = that the series N,N/2,&#x22EF;<= mo>,4,2,1" role=3D= "presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">N,N/,=E2=8B=AF,4,2,1N,N= /2,=E2=8B=AF,4= ,2,1 contains log2&#x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"= position: relative;">log= 2Nlog2=E2=81=A1N<= /span> elements since, if we start at N" role= =3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">= N<= span style=3D"display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: = 0.107em;">N and break the problem down in h= alf each time, it will take us approximately log2&#x2061;= N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;"= >log= 2Nlog2=E2=81=A1N steps to= completely reduce down to 1.

    Plugging into the formula, we get 2log2&#x2061;N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">2log2N2log2=E2=81=A1N nodes which simplifies to N" role=3D"presentation" style=3D"position: relative;">NN.     F. Conclusion

    Summary

    In this section, we learned about two different techniques for analyzing= the runtime of program.

    1. First, we tried to estimate the runtime efficiency of a program by timing it. But we found that the results weren't always the = same between different computers or even running at different times.

    2. Second, we learned how to estimate the efficiency= of an algorithm using proportionality and orders of growth.

      • big-Oh describes an upper bound for an algorithm's runtime= .
      • big-Omega describes a lower bound for an algorithm's runti= me.
      • big-Theta describes a tight bound for an algorithm's runti= me.

    In the final section, we applied what we learned about estimation and or= ders of growth to model algorithmic analysis for larger problems. Two techn= iques, graphs and call trees, helped us break down challe= nging problems into smaller pieces that we could analyze individually.

    Practical Tips

    1. Before attempting to calculate a function's runtime, first try to und= erstand what the function does.
    2. Try some small sample inputs to get a better intuition of what the fu= nction's runtime is like. What is the function doing with the input? How do= es the runtime change as the input size increases? Can you spot any 'gotcha= s' in the code that might invalidate our findings for larger inputs?
    3. Try to lower bound and upper bound the function runtime given what yo= u know about how the function works. This is a good sanity check for your l= ater observations.
    4. If the function is recursive, draw a call tree to map out the recursi= ve calls. This breaks the problem down into smaller parts that we can analy= ze individually. Once each part of the tree has been analyzed, we can then = reassemble all the parts to determine the overall runtime of the function.<= /li>
    5. If the function has a complicated loop, draw a bar graph to map out h= ow much work the body of the loop executes for each iteration.
